<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Technology" title="Portal:Technology">Article indices</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_technology_sidebar" title="Template:History of technology sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_technology_sidebar" title="Template talk:History of technology sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_technology_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Bronze Age</b> is a historic period, approximately 3300 BC to 1200 BC, that was characterized by the use of <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, in some areas <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>, and other early features of urban <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a>. The Bronze Age is the second principal period of the <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">three-age system</a>, as proposed in 1836 by <a href="/wiki/Christian_J%C3%BCrgensen_Thomsen" title="Christian Jürgensen Thomsen">Christian Jürgensen Thomsen</a>, for classifying and studying ancient societies and history.
</p><p>An ancient civilization is deemed to be part of the Bronze Age because it either produced bronze by <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> its own <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and alloying it with <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, or other metals, or traded other items for bronze from production areas elsewhere. Bronze was harder and more durable than other metals available at the time, allowing Bronze Age civilizations to gain a technological advantage.
</p><p>While terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> is naturally abundant, the higher temperature required for smelting, 1,250 °C (2,280 °F), in addition to the greater difficulty of working with the metal, placed it out of reach of common use until the end of the second millennium BC. Tin's low melting point of 231.9 °C (449.4 °F) and copper's relatively moderate melting point of 1,085 °C (1,985 °F) placed them within the capabilities of the Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kilns</a>, which date back to 6,000 BC and were able to produce temperatures greater than 900 °C (1,650 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IIIDorn2006_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-IIIDorn2006-1">[1]</a></sup> Copper and tin ores are rare, since there were no tin bronzes in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> before trading in bronze began in the <a href="/wiki/3rd_millennium_BC" title="3rd millennium BC">3rd millennium BC</a>. Worldwide, the Bronze Age generally followed the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period, with the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> serving as a transition.
</p><p>Bronze Age cultures differed in their <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">development of writing</a>. According to archaeological evidence, cultures in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> script) and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hieroglyph" title="Hieroglyph">hieroglyphs</a>) developed the earliest practical writing systems.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metallurgical_diffusion.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1475" data-file-width="2225" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Metallurgical_diffusion.svg/330px-Metallurgical_diffusion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Metallurgical_diffusion.svg/495px-Metallurgical_diffusion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Metallurgical_diffusion.svg/660px-Metallurgical_diffusion.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metallurgical_diffusion.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diffusion of <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>—the darkest areas are the oldest.</div></div></div>
<p>The overall period is characterized by the widespread use of bronze, though the place and time of the introduction and development of bronze technology were not universally synchronous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Human-made tin bronze technology requires set production techniques. Tin must be mined (mainly as the tin ore <a href="/wiki/Cassiterite" title="Cassiterite">cassiterite</a>) and smelted separately, then added to hot copper to make bronze alloy. The Bronze Age was a time of extensive use of metals and of developing trade networks (See <i><a href="/wiki/Tin_sources_and_trade_in_ancient_times" title="Tin sources and trade in ancient times">Tin sources and trade in ancient times</a></i>). A 2013 report suggests that the earliest tin-alloy bronze dates to the mid-5th millennium BC in a <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča culture</a> site in <a href="/wiki/Plo%C4%8Dnik_(archaeological_site)" title="Pločnik (archaeological site)">Pločnik</a> (<a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>), although this culture is not conventionally considered part of the Bronze Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jovanovic_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jovanovic-3">[3]</a></sup> The dating of the foil has been disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_East">Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> were the first regions to enter the Bronze Age, which began with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> civilization of <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> in the mid-4th millennium BC. Cultures in the ancient Near East (often called one of "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cradles_of_civilization" title="Cradles of civilization">cradles of civilization</a>") practiced intensive year-round agriculture, developed <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a>, invented the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter's wheel">potter's wheel</a>, created centralized governments (usually in form of hereditary monarchies), written law codes, <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> and nation-states and empires, embarked on advanced architectural projects, introduced <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a>, economic and civil administration, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, and practiced organized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a>, medicine and religion. Societies in the region laid the foundations for <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>.
<span class="anchor" id="Periodization"></span>
</p>
<dl><dd><i> Dates are approximate, consult particular article for details</i></dd></dl>
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_6zogemiqrjg8pn8hv18zx8rumutgk21"><area alt="New Kingdom of Egypt" coords="644,77,741,98" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" shape="rect" title="New Kingdom of Egypt"/><area alt="Middle Kingdom of Egypt" coords="473,77,587,98" href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" shape="rect" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt"/><area alt="Old Kingdom of Egypt" coords="319,77,416,98" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" shape="rect" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt"/><area alt="Early Dynastic Period of Egypt" coords="212,77,287,98" href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_of_Egypt" shape="rect" title="Early Dynastic Period of Egypt"/><area alt="Naqada III" coords="123,77,231,98" href="/wiki/Naqada_III" shape="rect" title="Naqada III"/><area alt="Ancient Egypt" coords="401,67,509,88" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" shape="rect" title="Ancient Egypt"/><area alt="Kassites" coords="627,53,707,73" href="/wiki/Kassites" shape="rect" title="Kassites"/><area alt="Babylonia" coords="494,53,580,73" href="/wiki/Babylonia" shape="rect" title="Babylonia"/><area alt="Assyria" coords="493,43,568,63" href="/wiki/Assyria" shape="rect" title="Assyria"/><area alt="Third Dynasty of Ur" coords="453,53,500,73" href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" shape="rect" title="Third Dynasty of Ur"/><area alt="Akkadian Empire" coords="378,53,453,73" href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" shape="rect" title="Akkadian Empire"/><area alt="Cities of the ancient Near East" coords="240,53,388,73" href="/wiki/Cities_of_the_ancient_Near_East" shape="rect" title="Cities of the ancient Near East"/><area alt="Ancient Near East" coords="357,18,487,38" href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" shape="rect" title="Ancient Near East"/></map><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/6zogemiqrjg8pn8hv18zx8rumutgk21.png" usemap="#timeline_6zogemiqrjg8pn8hv18zx8rumutgk21"/></div>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin:0 0 1em 1em; width:300px; text-align:left; clear:right;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="background:#f8eaba; text-align:center;">
<center>
<dl><dt>Near East Bronze Age Divisions</dt></dl>
</center>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>
<p>The Bronze Age in the Near East can be conveniently divided into Early, Middle and Late periods. The dates and phases below are solely applicable to the Near East and thus not applicable universally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt><i>Early Bronze Age</i> (EBA)</dt></dl>
<p>3300–2100 BC
</p>
<dl><dd>3300–3000: EBA I</dd>
<dd>3000–2700: EBA II</dd>
<dd>2700–2200: EBA III</dd>
<dd>2200–2100: EBA IV</dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>Middle Bronze Age</i> (MBA)</dt>
<dd>Also, <i>Intermediate Bronze Age</i> (IBA)</dd></dl>
<p>2100–1550 BC
</p>
<dl><dd>2100–2000: MBA I</dd>
<dd>2000–1750: MBA II A</dd>
<dd>1750–1650: MBA II B</dd>
<dd>1650–1550: MBA II C</dd></dl>
<dl><dt><i>Late Bronze Age</i> (LBA)</dt></dl>
<p>1550–1200 BC
</p>
<dl><dd>1550–1400: LBA I</dd>
<dd>1400–1300: LBA II A</dd>
<dd>1300–1200: LBA II B (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a>)</dd></dl>
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatolia">Anatolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Anatolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Anatolia#Bronze_Age" title="Prehistory of Anatolia">Prehistory of Anatolia § Bronze Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG/170px-Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG/255px-Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG/340px-Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hattusa_Bronze_Tablet_Cuneiform.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hittite bronze tablet from Çorum-Boğazköy dating from 1235 BC, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations" title="Museum of Anatolian Civilizations">Museum of Anatolian Civilizations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a> in northern Anatolia from the 18th century BC. In the 14th century BC the Hittite Kingdom was at its height, encompassing central Anatolia, southwestern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> as far as <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a>, and upper <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. After 1180 BC, amid general turmoil in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> conjectured to have been associated with the sudden arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AK2013_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-AK2013-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drews48_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drews48-10">[10]</a></sup> the kingdom disintegrated into several independent "Neo-Hittite" city-states, some of which survived until as late as the 8th century BC.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arzawa" title="Arzawa">Arzawa</a> in Western Anatolia during the second half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_millennium_BC" title="Second millennium BC">second millennium BC</a> likely extended along southern Anatolia in a belt that reaches from near the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Lakes_Region" title="Turkish Lakes Region">Turkish Lakes Region</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> coast. <a href="/wiki/Arzawa" title="Arzawa">Arzawa</a> was the western neighbor—sometimes a rival and sometimes a vassal—of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_Kingdom" title="Hittite Kingdom">Middle and New Hittite Kingdoms</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assuwa_league" title="Assuwa league">Assuwa league</a> was a confederation of states in western Anatolia that was defeated by the Hittites under an earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tudhaliya_I" title="Tudhaliya I">Tudhaliya I</a>, around 1400 BC. Arzawa has been associated with the much more obscure <a href="/wiki/Assuwa" title="Assuwa">Assuwa</a> generally located to its north. It probably bordered it, and may even be an alternative term for it (at least during some periods).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Bronze_dynasties">Early Bronze dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Bronze dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egypt,_New_Kingdom,_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5549" data-file-width="3354" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Egypt%2C_New_Kingdom%2C_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egypt,_New_Kingdom,_Dynasty_18_-_Caryatid_Mirror_-_1983.196_-_Cleveland_Museum_of_Art.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">mirror</a> with a female human figure at the base, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eighteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt">Eighteenth dynasty of Egypt</a> (1540–1296 BC)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="858" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg/170px-Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg/255px-Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg/340px-Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thutmose_III_sphinx_E10897-Louvre_042005_06.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sphinx-lion of <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a> 1479–1425 BC</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, the Bronze Age begins in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protodynastic_Period_of_Egypt" title="Protodynastic Period of Egypt">Protodynastic period</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3150 BC. The archaic <i>Early Bronze Age of Egypt</i>, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_of_Egypt" title="Early Dynastic Period of Egypt">Early Dynastic Period of Egypt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSnPG_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-KSnPG-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> immediately follows the unification of Lower and Upper Egypt, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3100 BC. It is generally taken to include the First and Second Dynasties, lasting from the Protodynastic Period of Egypt until about 2686 BC, or the beginning of the Old Kingdom. With the First Dynasty, the capital moved from <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Egypt" title="Abydos, Egypt">Abydos</a> to Memphis with a unified Egypt ruled by an Egyptian god-king. Abydos remained the major holy land in the south. The hallmarks of ancient Egyptian civilization, such as art, architecture and many aspects of religion, took shape during the Early Dynastic Period. <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a> in the Early Bronze Age was the largest city of the time.
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">Old Kingdom</a> of the regional Bronze Age<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSnPG_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-KSnPG-11">[11]</a></sup> is the name given to the period in the 3rd millennium BC when Egypt attained its first continuous peak of civilization in complexity and achievement – the first of three "Kingdom" periods, which mark the high points of civilization in the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Egypt#Nile_Valley_and_Delta" title="Geography of Egypt">lower Nile Valley</a> (the others being <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">New Kingdom</a>).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="First Intermediate Period of Egypt">First Intermediate Period of Egypt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> often described as a "dark period" in ancient Egyptian history, spanned about 100 years after the end of the Old Kingdom from about 2181 to 2055 BC. Very little monumental evidence survives from this period, especially from the early part of it. The First Intermediate Period was a dynamic time when the rule of Egypt was roughly divided between two competing for power bases: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herakleopolis_Magna" title="Herakleopolis Magna">Heracleopolis</a> in Lower Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a> in Upper Egypt. These two kingdoms would eventually come into conflict, with the Theban kings conquering the north, resulting in the reunification of Egypt under a single ruler during the second part of the 11th Dynasty.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nubia">Nubia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nubia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The Bronze Age in <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> started as early as 2300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-14">[14]</a></sup> Copper smelting was introduced by Egyptians to the Nubian city of <a href="/wiki/Mero%C3%AB" title="Meroë">Meroë</a>, in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, around 2600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-15">[15]</a></sup> A furnace for bronze casting has been found in <a href="/wiki/Kerma" title="Kerma">Kerma</a> that is dated to 2300–1900 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Bronze_dynasties">Middle Bronze dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Middle Bronze dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom of Egypt</a> lasted from 2055 to 1650 BC. During this period, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Osiris funerary cult</a> rose to dominate Egyptian popular religion. The period comprises two phases: the 11th Dynasty, which ruled from Thebes and the 12th<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and 13th Dynasties centered on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El-Lisht" title="El-Lisht">el-Lisht</a>. The unified kingdom was previously considered to comprise the 11th and 12th Dynasties, but historians now at least partially consider the 13th Dynasty to belong to the Middle Kingdom.
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period" title="Second Intermediate Period">Second Intermediate Period</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Ancient Egypt fell into disarray for a second time, between the end of the Middle Kingdom and the start of the New Kingdom. It is best known for the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a>, whose reign comprised the 15th and 16th dynasties. The Hyksos first appeared in Egypt during the 11th Dynasty, began their climb to power in the 13th Dynasty, and emerged from the Second Intermediate Period in control of <a href="/wiki/Avaris" title="Avaris">Avaris</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Delta</a>. By the 15th Dynasty, they ruled lower Egypt, and they were expelled at the end of the 17th Dynasty.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Bronze_dynasties">Late Bronze dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Late Bronze dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom of Egypt</a>, also referred to as the Egyptian Empire, lasted from the 16th to the 11th century BC. The New Kingdom followed the Second Intermediate Period and was succeeded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Intermediate_Period" title="Third Intermediate Period">Third Intermediate Period</a>. It was Egypt's most prosperous time and marked the peak of Egypt's power. The later New Kingdom, i.e. the 19th and 20th Dynasties (1292–1069 BC), is also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramesside_period" title="Ramesside period">Ramesside period</a>, after the eleven pharaohs that took the name of Ramesses.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iranian_Plateau">Iranian Plateau</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Iranian Plateau">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elam_cool.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="368" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Elam_cool.jpg/170px-Elam_cool.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Elam_cool.jpg/255px-Elam_cool.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Elam_cool.jpg/340px-Elam_cool.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elam_cool.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late 3rd Millennium BC silver cup from Marvdasht, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars</a>, with linear-Elamite inscription.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> was a <i>pre-Iranian</i> ancient civilization located to the east of Mesopotamia. In the Old Elamite period (Middle Bronze Age), Elam consisted of kingdoms on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a>, centered in <a href="/wiki/Anshan_(Persia)" title="Anshan (Persia)">Anshan</a>, and from the mid-2nd millennium BC, it was centered in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzestan" title="Khuzestan">Khuzestan</a> lowlands. Its culture played a crucial role in the <a href="/wiki/Gutian_people" title="Gutian people">Gutian Empire</a> and especially during the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_dynasty" title="Achaemenid dynasty">Achaemenid dynasty</a> that succeeded it.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Oxus civilization</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> was a Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> culture dated to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2300–1700 BC and centered on the upper <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> (Oxus). In the Early Bronze Age, the culture of the <a href="/wiki/Kopet_Dag" title="Kopet Dag">Kopet Dag</a> oases and <a href="/wiki/Altyndepe" title="Altyndepe">Altyndepe</a> developed a proto-urban society. This corresponds to level IV at <a href="/wiki/Namazga-Tepe" title="Namazga-Tepe">Namazga-Tepe</a>. Altyndepe was a major center even then. Pottery was wheel-turned. Grapes were grown. The height of this urban development was reached in the Middle Bronze Age <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2300 BC, corresponding to level V at Namazga-Depe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This Bronze Age culture is called the <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</a> (BMAC).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kulli_culture" title="Kulli culture">Kulli culture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> similar to those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a>, was located in southern <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a> (Gedrosia) <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2500–2000 BC. Agriculture was the economic base of these people. At several places, dams were found, providing evidence for a highly developed water management system.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chlorite_object_Jiroft,_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE,_Bronze_Age_I,_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg/220px-Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg/330px-Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg/440px-Chlorite_object_Jiroft%2C_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE%2C_Bronze_Age_I%2C_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chlorite_object_Jiroft,_Kerman_ca._2500_BCE,_Bronze_Age_I,_National_Museum_of_Iran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Master_of_Animals" title="Master of Animals">Master of Animals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chlorite_group" title="Chlorite group">chlorite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiroft_culture" title="Jiroft culture">Jiroft culture</a>, c. 2500 BC, Bronze Age I, <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Iran" title="National Museum of Iran">National Museum of Iran</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Konar_Sandal" title="Konar Sandal">Konar Sandal</a> is associated with the hypothesized "<a href="/wiki/Jiroft_culture" title="Jiroft culture">Jiroft culture</a>", a 3rd-millennium-BC culture postulated based on a collection of artifacts confiscated in 2001.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Levant">Levant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Levant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/220px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/330px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/440px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chalcolithic copper mine in <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negev_Desert" title="Negev Desert">Negev Desert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_ancient_Levant#Bronze_Age" title="History of the ancient Levant">History of the ancient Levant § Bronze Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_the_Levant" title="Prehistory of the Levant">Prehistory of the Levant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_archaeological_periods_(Levant)" title="List of archaeological periods (Levant)">List of archaeological periods (Levant)</a></div>
<p>In modern scholarship, the chronology of the Bronze Age Levant is divided into Early/Proto Syrian; corresponding to the Early Bronze. Old Syrian; corresponding to the Middle Bronze. Middle Syrian; corresponding to the Late Bronze. The term Neo-Syria is used to designate the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The old Syrian period was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Ebla#First_kingdom" title="Ebla">Eblaite first kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak#Kingdom_of_Nagar" title="Tell Brak">Nagar</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_second_kingdom_of_Mari" title="The second kingdom of Mari">Mariote second kingdom</a>. The Akkadians conquered large areas of the Levant and were followed by the <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorite kingdoms</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2000–1600 BC, which arose in <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria#The_Lim_dynasty" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamhad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatna" title="Qatna">Qatna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> From the 15th century BC onward, the term <a href="/wiki/Amurru_kingdom" title="Amurru kingdom">Amurru</a> is usually applied to the region extending north of Canaan as far as <a href="/wiki/Kadesh_(Syria)" title="Kadesh (Syria)">Kadesh</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Orontes_River" title="Orontes River">Orontes River</a>.
</p><p>The earliest-known <a href="/wiki/Ugaritic" title="Ugaritic">Ugaritic</a> contact with Egypt (and the first exact dating of Ugaritic civilization) comes from a <a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a> bead identified with the Middle Kingdom pharaoh Senusret I, 1971–1926 BC. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stela" title="Stela">stela</a> and a statuette from the Egyptian pharaohs Senusret III and Amenemhet III have also been found. However, it is unclear at what time these monuments got to Ugarit. In the <a href="/wiki/Amarna_letters" title="Amarna letters">Amarna letters</a>, messages from Ugarit <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1350 BC written by Ammittamru I, Niqmaddu II, and his queen, were discovered. From the 16th to the 13th century BC, Ugarit remained in constant touch with Egypt and Cyprus (named Alashiya).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> was a loosely organized state in northern Syria and south-east Anatolia from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500–1300 BC. Founded by an Indo-Aryan ruling class that governed a predominantly Hurrian population, Mitanni came to be a regional power after the Hittite destruction of Kassite Babylon created a power vacuum in Mesopotamia. At its beginning, Mitanni's major rival was Egypt under the Thutmosids. However, with the ascent of the Hittite empire, Mitanni and Egypt allied to protect their mutual interests from the threat of Hittite domination. At the height of its power, during the 14th century BC, it had outposts centered on its capital, <a href="/wiki/Washukanni" title="Washukanni">Washukanni</a>, which archaeologists have located on the headwaters of the Khabur River. Eventually, Mitanni succumbed to Hittite, and later Assyrian attacks, and was reduced to a province of the Middle Assyrian Empire.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> were an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Semitic-speaking_peoples" title="Ancient Semitic-speaking peoples">ancient Semitic-speaking people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a> who inhabited part of <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> during the <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">tribal and monarchic periods</a> (15th to 6th centuries BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and lived in the region in smaller numbers after the fall of the monarchy. The name "Israel" first appears <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1209 BC, at the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a> and the very beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Merneptah_Stele" title="Merneptah Stele">Merneptah Stele</a> raised by the Egyptian pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Merneptah" title="Merneptah">Merneptah</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Aramaeans</a> were a Northwest Semitic semi-nomadic and pastoralist people who originated in what is now modern Syria (Biblical Aram) during the Late Bronze Age and the early Iron Age. Large groups migrated to Mesopotamia, where they intermingled with the native Akkadian (Assyrian and Babylonian) population. The Aramaeans never had a unified empire; they were divided into independent kingdoms all across the Near East. After the Bronze Age collapse, their political influence was confined to many Syro-Hittite states, which were entirely absorbed into the Neo-Assyrian Empire by the 8th century BC.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mesopotamia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mesopotamia" title="History of Mesopotamia">History of Mesopotamia</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Mesopotamia" title="Bronze Age Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian Bronze Age</a> began about 3500 BC and ended with the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite</a> period (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 BC – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1155 BC). The usual tripartite division into an Early, Middle and Late Bronze Age is not used. Instead, a division primarily based on art-historical and historical characteristics is more common.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cities_of_the_Ancient_Near_East" title="Cities of the Ancient Near East">cities of the Ancient Near East</a> housed several tens of thousands of people. <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a> in the Middle Bronze Age and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calah" title="Calah">Calah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> in the Late Bronze Age similarly had large populations. The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> (2335–2154 BC) became the dominant power in the region, and after its fall the Sumerians enjoyed a renaissance with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">Neo-Sumerian Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> became a regional power, under the Amorite king <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">Shamshi-Adad I</a>, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1800–1600 BC). The earliest mention of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> (then a small administrative town) appears on a tablet from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a> in the 23rd century BC. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> dynasty established the <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a> of Babylon in the 19th century BC. Over 100 years later, it briefly took over the other city-states and formed the short-lived First <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> Empire during what is also called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_dynasty" title="First Babylonian dynasty">Old Babylonian Period</a>. Akkad, Assyria, and Babylonia all used the written <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">East Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> for official use and as a spoken language. By that time, the Sumerian language was no longer spoken, but was still in religious use in Assyria and Babylonia, and would remain so until the 1st century AD. The <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> traditions played a major role in later Assyrian and Babylonian culture, even though Babylonia (unlike the more militarily powerful Assyria) itself was founded by non-native <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a> and often ruled by other non-indigenous peoples, such as <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Aramaeans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>, as well as its Assyrian neighbors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_in_2000_BC.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2234" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/World_in_2000_BC.svg/330px-World_in_2000_BC.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/World_in_2000_BC.svg/495px-World_in_2000_BC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/World_in_2000_BC.svg/660px-World_in_2000_BC.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_in_2000_BC.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the world in 2000 BC</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Agropastoralism">Agropastoralism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Agropastoralism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>For many decades scholars made superficial reference to Central Asia as the "pastoral realm" or alternatively, the "nomadic world", in what researchers have come to call the "Central Asian void": a 5,000 year span that was neglected in studies of the origins of agriculture. Foothill regions and glacial melt streams supported Bronze Age agropastoralists who developed complex east–west trade routes between Central Asia and China that introduced wheat and barley to China and spread millet across Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="Bactria.E2.80.93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bactria–Margiana_Archaeological_Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</a> (BMAC), also known as the Oxus civilization, was a Bronze Age civilization in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, dated to c. 2400–1600 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> located in present-day northern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, centred on the upper <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> (Oxus River). Its sites were discovered and named by the Soviet archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Sarianidi" title="Viktor Sarianidi">Viktor Sarianidi</a> (1976). <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> was the Greek name for the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bactra" title="Bactra">Bactra</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>), in what is now northern Afghanistan, and <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a> was the Greek name for the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrapy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Marguš</a>, the capital of which was <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a>, in modern-day southeastern Turkmenistan.
</p><p>A wealth of information indicates that the BMAC had close international relations with the Indus Valley, the Iranian Plateau, and possibly even indirectly with Mesopotamia, and all civilizations were very familiar with lost wax casting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to recent studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> the BMAC was not a primary contributor to later South-Asian genetics.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Seima-Turbino_phenomenon">Seima-Turbino phenomenon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Seima-Turbino phenomenon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">Seima-Turbino phenomenon</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a> in what is now southern <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> have been identified as the point of origin of a cultural enigma termed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_Phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino Phenomenon">Seima-Turbino Phenomenon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKeys_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKeys-33">[33]</a></sup> It is conjectured that changes in climate in this region around 2000 BC and the ensuing ecological, economic and political changes triggered a rapid and massive migration westward into northeast Europe, eastward into China and southward into <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-White-34">[34]</a></sup> across a frontier of some 4,000 miles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKeys_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKeys-33">[33]</a></sup> This migration took place in just five to six generations and led to peoples from Finland in the west to Thailand in the east employing the same metal working technology and, in some areas, horse breeding and riding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKeys_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKeys-33">[33]</a></sup> It is further conjectured that the same migrations spread the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a> group of languages across Europe and Asia: some 39 languages of this group are still extant, including <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCKeys_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-BBCKeys-33">[33]</a></sup> However, recent genetic testings of sites in south Siberia and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andronovo" title="Andronovo">Andronovo</a> horizon) would rather support a spreading of the bronze technology via <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a> eastwards, as this technology was well known for quite a while in western regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gefuding_Gui.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gefuding_Gui.jpg/220px-Gefuding_Gui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gefuding_Gui.jpg/330px-Gefuding_Gui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gefuding_Gui.jpg/440px-Gefuding_Gui.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gefuding_Gui.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> two-handled bronze <i>gefuding gui</i> (1600–1046 BC)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg/220px-Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg/330px-Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg/440px-Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pu_with_openwork_interlaced_dragons_design.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> <i>pu</i> bronze vessel with interlaced <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragon</a> design</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Erlitou_culture" title="Erlitou culture">Erlitou culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sanxingdui" title="Sanxingdui">Sanxingdui</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Xiajiadian_culture" title="Lower Xiajiadian culture">Lower Xiajiadian culture</a></div>
<p>In China, the earliest bronze artifacts have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Majiayao_culture" title="Majiayao culture">Majiayao culture</a> site (between 3100 and 2700 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Bronze Age" has been transferred to the archaeology of China from that of Western Eurasia, and there is no consensus or universally used convention delimiting the "Bronze Age" in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_prehistory" title="Chinese prehistory">Chinese prehistory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>By convention, the "Early Bronze Age" in China is sometimes taken as equivalent to the "<a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>" period (16th to 11th centuries BC),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and the "Later Bronze Age" as equivalent to the "<a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>" period (11th to 3rd centuries BC, from the 5th century, also dubbed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_China" title="Iron Age China">Iron Age</a>"), although there is an argument to be made that the "Bronze Age" proper never ended in China, as there is no recognizable transition to an "Iron Age".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Significantly, together with the jade art that precedes it, bronze was seen as a "fine" material for ritual art when compared with iron or stone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bronze <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> in China originated in what is referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erlitou" title="Erlitou">Erlitou</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Erh-li-t'ou</span></i>) period, which some historians argue places it within the range of dates controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Others believe the Erlitou sites belong to the preceding <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">Hsia</span></i>) dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a> defines the Chinese Bronze Age as the "period between about 2000 BC and 771 BC", a period that begins with the Erlitou culture and ends abruptly with the disintegration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Zhou_dynasty" title="Western Zhou dynasty">Western Zhou</a> rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is reason to believe that bronze work developed inside China separately from outside influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> However, the discovery of Europoid <a href="/wiki/Tarim_mummies" title="Tarim mummies">mummies in Xinjiang</a> has caused some scholars such as Johan Gunnar Andersson, Jan Romgard, and An Zhimin to suggest a possible route of transmission from the West eastwards. According to An Zhimin, "It can be imagined that initially bronze and iron technology took its rise in West Asia, first influenced the Xinjiang region, and then reached the Yellow River valley, providing external impetus for the rise of the Shang and Zhou civilizations." According to Jan Romgard, "bronze and iron tools seems to have traveled from west to east as well as the use of wheeled wagons and the domestication of the horse." There are also possible links to Seima-Turbino culture, "a transcultural complex across northern Eurasia," the Eurasian steppe, and the Urals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> However the oldest bronze objects found in China so far were discovered at the Majiayao site in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> rather than at <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (also known as the Yin dynasty)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_River_Valley" title="Yellow River Valley">Yellow River Valley</a> rose to power after the <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia dynasty</a> around 1600 BC. While some direct information about the Shang dynasty comes from Shang-era inscriptions on bronze artifacts, most comes from oracle bones—turtle shells, cattle scapulae, or other bones—which bear <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a> that form the first significant corpus of recorded Chinese characters.
</p><p>The production of <a href="/wiki/Erlitou_culture" title="Erlitou culture">Erlitou</a> in Henan represents the earliest large-scale metallurgy industry in the Central Plains of China. The influence of the <a href="/wiki/Seima-Turbino_phenomenon" title="Seima-Turbino phenomenon">Saima-Turbino</a> metalworking tradition from the north is supported by a series of recent discoveries in China of many unique perforated spearheads with downward hooks and small loops on the same or opposite side of the socket, which could be associated with the Seima-Turbino visual vocabulary of southern Siberia. The metallurgical centers of northwestern China, especially Qijia in Gansu and Kexingzhuang culture in Shaanxi, played an intermediary role in this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Iron has been found from the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, but its use was minimal. Chinese literature dating to the 6th century BC attests knowledge of iron smelting, yet bronze continues to occupy the seat of significance in the archaeological and historical record for some time after this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Historian W.C. White argues that iron did not supplant bronze "at any period before the end of the Zhou dynasty (256 BC)" and that bronze vessels make up the majority of metal vessels through the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Later Han period</a>, or to 221 BC [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a>?</i>].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chinese bronze artifacts generally are either utilitarian, like spear points or <a href="/wiki/Adze" title="Adze">adze</a> heads, or <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_bronzes" title="Chinese ritual bronzes">"ritual bronzes"</a>, which are more elaborate versions in precious materials of everyday vessels, as well as tools and weapons. Examples are the numerous large sacrificial tripods known as <a href="/wiki/Ding_(vessel)" title="Ding (vessel)">dings</a> in Chinese; there are many other distinct shapes. Surviving identified Chinese ritual bronzes tend to be highly decorated, often with the <i><a href="/wiki/Taotie" title="Taotie">taotie</a></i> motif, which involves highly stylized animal faces. These appear in three main motif types: those of demons, of symbolic animals, and abstract symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Many large bronzes also bear <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">cast inscriptions</a> that are the great bulk of the surviving body of early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_writing" title="Chinese writing">Chinese writing</a> and have helped historians and archaeologists piece together the history of China, especially during the Zhou dynasty (1046–256 BC).
</p><p>The bronzes of the Western Zhou dynasty document large portions of history not found in the extant texts that were often composed by persons of varying rank and possibly even social class. Further, the medium of cast bronze lends the record they preserve a permanence not enjoyed by manuscripts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> These inscriptions can commonly be subdivided into four parts: a reference to the date and place, the naming of the event commemorated, the list of gifts given to the artisan in exchange for the bronze, and a dedication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The relative points of reference these vessels provide have enabled historians to place most of the vessels within a certain time frame of the Western Zhou period, allowing them to trace the evolution of the vessels and the events they record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<table align="right">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="color:#black; background:#f8eaba; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Korean Bronze Age
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:30px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg" title="Bronze artifacts from Daegok-ri, Hwasun, Korea"><img alt="Bronze artifacts from Daegok-ri, Hwasun, Korea" data-file-height="2242" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg/120px-%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg/180px-%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg/240px-%ED%99%94%EC%88%9C_%EB%8C%80%EA%B3%A1%EB%A6%AC_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%9D%BC%EA%B4%84.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mumun_pottery_period" title="Mumun pottery period">Mumun pottery period</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="extiw" href="https://ko.wikipedia.org/wiki/%ED%95%9C%EA%B5%AD%EC%9D%98_%EC%B2%AD%EB%8F%99%EA%B8%B0_%EC%8B%9C%EB%8C%80" title="ko:한국의 청동기 시대">ko:한국의 청동기 시대</a></div>
<p>The beginning of the Bronze Age on the peninsula is around 1000–800 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Initially centered around <a href="/wiki/Liaoning_bronze_dagger_culture" title="Liaoning bronze dagger culture">Liaoning</a> and southern Manchuria, Korean Bronze Age culture exhibits unique typology and styles, especially in ritual objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mumun_pottery_period" title="Mumun pottery period">Mumun pottery period</a> is named after the Korean name for undecorated or plain cooking and storage vessels that form a large part of the pottery assemblage over the entire length of the period, but especially 850–550 BC. The Mumun period is known for the origins of intensive agriculture and complex societies in both the Korean Peninsula and the Japanese Archipelago.
</p><p>The Middle Mumun pottery period culture of the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> gradually adopted bronze production (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 700–600? BC) after a period when Liaoning-style bronze daggers and other bronze artifacts were exchanged as far as the interior part of the Southern Peninsula (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 900–700 BC). The bronze daggers lent prestige and authority to the personages who wielded and were buried with them in high-status megalithic burials at south-coastal centers such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igeum-dong_site" title="Igeum-dong site">Igeum-dong site</a>. Bronze was an important element in ceremonies and as for mortuary offerings until 100 BC.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<table align="right">
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="color:#black; background:#f8eaba; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">Japanese Bronze Age
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_(1_of_2).jpg" title="2nd century BC Yayoi dōtaku bronze bell."><img alt="2nd century BC Yayoi dōtaku bronze bell." data-file-height="850" data-file-width="527" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg/74px-Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg/111px-Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg/149px-Dotaku_LACMA_M.58.9.3_%281_of_2%29.jpg 2x" width="74"/></a></div></div>

</p>
