</td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a></div>
<p>The Japanese archipelago saw the introduction of bronze during the beginning of the Early <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a> (≈300 BC), which saw the introduction of metalworking and agricultural practices brought in by settlers arriving from the continent. Bronze and iron smelting techniques spread to the Japanese archipelago through contact with other ancient East Asian civilizations, particularly immigration and trade from ancient Korean peninsula and ancient mainland China. Iron was mainly used for agricultural and other tools, whereas ritual and ceremonial artifacts were mainly made of bronze.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>(Dates are approximate, consult particular article for details)
</p>
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map name="timeline_7ffn9azexes25rsk84iukhpxfz5ydaf"><area alt="Cemetery H culture" coords="504,9,606,30" href="/wiki/Cemetery_H_culture" shape="rect" title="Cemetery H culture"/><area alt="Mature Harappan" coords="315,9,435,30" href="/wiki/Mature_Harappan" shape="rect" title="Mature Harappan"/><area alt="Indus Valley Civilization" coords="107,9,205,30" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" shape="rect" title="Indus Valley Civilization"/><area alt="Bronze Age India" coords="273,-3,398,18" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_India" shape="rect" title="Bronze Age India"/></map><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/7ffn9azexes25rsk84iukhpxfz5ydaf.png" usemap="#timeline_7ffn9azexes25rsk84iukhpxfz5ydaf"/></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Indus_Valley">Indus Valley</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Indus Valley">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2530" data-file-width="1404" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/170px-Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/255px-Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/340px-Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_Girl_of_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dancing_Girl_(sculpture)" title="Dancing Girl (sculpture)"><i>Dancing girl</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>, c. 2500 BC (replica).</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a></div>
<p>The Bronze Age on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> began around 3300 BC with the beginning of the Indus Valley civilization. Inhabitants of the Indus Valley, the <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappans</a>, developed new techniques in metallurgy and produced copper, bronze, lead and tin. The Late Harappan culture, which dates from 1900 to 1400 BC, overlapped the transition from the Bronze Age to the Iron Age; thus it is difficult to date this transition accurately. It has been claimed that a 6,000-year-old copper <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulet</a> manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> in the shape of wheel spoke is the earliest example of <a href="/wiki/Lost-wax_casting" title="Lost-wax casting">lost-wax casting</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The civilization's cities were noted for their urban planning, baked brick houses, elaborate drainage systems, water supply systems, clusters of large non-residential buildings, and new techniques in handicraft (carnelian products, seal carving) and metallurgy (copper, bronze, lead, and tin).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The large cities of Mohenjo-daro and Harappa very likely grew to contain between 30,000 and 60,000 individuals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and the civilization itself during its florescence may have contained between one and five million individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Vilabouly_Complex,_Laos" title="Vilabouly Complex, Laos">Vilabouly Complex</a> in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> is a significant archaeological site for dating the origin of bronze metallurgy in Southeast Asia.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chiang" title="Ban Chiang">Ban Chiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>) bronze <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> have been discovered dating to 2100 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> However, according to the radiocarbon dating on the human and pig bones in Ban Chiang, some scholars propose that the initial Bronze Age in Ban Chiang was in late 2nd millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-66">[66]</a></sup> In Nyaunggan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, bronze tools have been excavated along with ceramics and stone artifacts. Dating is still currently broad (3500–500 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Ban Non Wat, excavated by Charles Higham, was a rich site with over 640 graves excavated that gleaned many complex bronze items that may have had social value connected to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ban Chiang, however, is the most thoroughly documented site while having the clearest evidence of metallurgy when it comes to Southeast Asia. With a rough date range of late 3rd millennium BC to the first millennium AD, this site alone has various artifacts such as burial pottery (dating from 2100 to 1700 BC), fragments of Bronze, copper-base bangles, and much more. What's interesting about this site, however, is not just the old age of the artifacts but that this technology suggested on-site casting from the very beginning. The on-site casting supports the theory that Bronze was first introduced in Southeast Asia as fully developed which therefore shows that Bronze was innovated from a different country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_1995_101–123_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-White_1995_101–123-69">[69]</a></sup> Some scholars believe that the copper-based metallurgy was disseminated from northwest and central China via south and southwest areas such as Guangdong province and Yunnan province and finally into southeast Asia around 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-66">[66]</a></sup> Archaeology also suggests that Bronze Age metallurgy may not have been as significant a catalyst in social stratification and warfare in Southeast Asia as in other regions, social distribution shifting away from chiefdom-states to a heterarchical network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_1995_101–123_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-White_1995_101–123-69">[69]</a></sup> Data analyses of sites such as Ban Lum Khao, Ban Na Di, Non-Nok Tha, Khok Phanom Di, and Nong Nor have consistently led researchers to conclude that there was no entrenched hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dong_Son_culture" title="Dong Son culture">Dong Son culture</a></div>
<p>Dating back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_Age" title="Neolithic Age">Neolithic Age</a>, the first bronze drum, called the <a href="/wiki/Dong_Son_drum" title="Dong Son drum">Dong Son drum</a>, were uncovered in and around the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Delta" title="Red River Delta">Red River Delta</a> regions of Northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and Southern China. These relate to the prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Dong_Son_culture" title="Dong Son culture">Dong Son culture</a> of Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological research in Northern Vietnam indicates an increase in rates of infectious disease following the advent of metallurgy; skeletal fragments in sites dating to the early and mid-Bronze Age evidence a greater proportion of lesions than in sites of earlier periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxenham_2005_359–376_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxenham_2005_359–376-72">[72]</a></sup> There are a few possible implications of this. One is the increased contact with bacterial and/or fungal pathogens due to increased population density and land clearing/cultivation. The other one is decreased levels of immunocompetence in the Metal age due to changes in the diet caused by agriculture. The last is that there may have been an emergence of infectious disease in the Da But the period that evolved into a more virulent form in the metal period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxenham_2005_359–376_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxenham_2005_359–376-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Myanmar">Myanmar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Myanmar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nyaung-gan" title="Nyaung-gan">Nyaung-gan</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Europe" title="Bronze Age Europe">Bronze Age Europe</a></div>

(Dates are approximate, consult particular article for details)
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aegean">Aegean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Aegean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aegean_civilization" title="Aegean civilization">Aegean civilization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:210px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1748" data-file-width="1748" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/208px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/312px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg/416px-MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg 2x" width="208"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MaskOfAgamemnon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold '<a href="/wiki/Mask_of_Agamemnon" title="Mask of Agamemnon">Mask of Agamemnon</a>' produced during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a>, Greece, 1550 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Aegean Bronze Age began around 3200 BC, when civilizations first established a far-ranging <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> network. This network imported <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and charcoal to <a href="/wiki/Alashiya" title="Alashiya">Cyprus</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> was mined and alloyed with the tin to produce bronze. Bronze objects were then exported far and wide and supported the trade. <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotopic</a> analysis of tin in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> bronze artifacts suggests that they may have originated from <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Britain" title="Bronze Age Britain">Great Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a> was well developed at this time and reached a peak of skill not exceeded (except perhaps by <a href="/wiki/Polynesia#Polynesian_navigation" title="Polynesia">Polynesian</a> sailors) until 1730 when the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">chronometer</a> enabled the precise determination of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> appears to have coordinated and defended its Bronze Age trade. Ancient empires valued <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxury_good" title="Luxury good">luxury goods</a> in contrast to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staple_foods" title="Staple foods">staple foods</a>, leading to famine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Aegean_collapse">Aegean collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Aegean collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_End.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="812" data-file-width="1151" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bronze_Age_End.svg/205px-Bronze_Age_End.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bronze_Age_End.svg/308px-Bronze_Age_End.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bronze_Age_End.svg/410px-Bronze_Age_End.svg.png 2x" width="205"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Age_End.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Invasions, destruction and possible population movements during the collapse of the Bronze Age, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1200 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Bronze Age collapse theories have described aspects of the end of the Bronze Age in this region. At the end of the Bronze Age in the Aegean region, the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> administration of the regional trade empire followed the decline of Minoan primacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Several Minoan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Client_states" title="Client states">client states</a> lost much of their population to famine and/or pestilence. This would indicate that the trade network may have failed, preventing the trade that would previously have relieved such famines and prevented illness caused by malnutrition. It is also known that in this era the <a href="/wiki/Breadbasket" title="Breadbasket">breadbasket</a> of the Minoan empire, the area north of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, also suddenly lost much of its population, and thus probably some capacity to cultivate crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Drought and famine in Anatolia may have also led to the Aegean collapse by disrupting trade networks, and therefore preventing the Aegean from accessing bronze and luxury goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Aegean collapse has been attributed to the exhaustion of the <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Cyprus" title="Geography of Cyprus">Cypriot</a> forests causing the end of the bronze trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> These forests are known to have existed into later times, and experiments have shown that <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> production on the scale necessary for the bronze production of the late Bronze Age would have exhausted them in less than fifty years.
</p><p>The Aegean collapse has also been attributed to the fact that as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tools" title="Tools">tools</a> became more common, the main justification for the tin trade ended, and that trade network ceased to function as it did formerly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The colonies of the Minoan empire then suffered drought, famine, war, or some combination of those three, and had no access to the distant resources of an empire by which they could easily recover.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera_eruption" title="Thera eruption">Thera eruption</a> occurred <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC, 110 km (68 mi) north of Crete. Speculation includes that a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> from Thera (more commonly known today as <a href="/wiki/Santorini" title="Santorini">Santorini</a>) destroyed Cretan cities. A tsunami may have destroyed the Cretan <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">navy</a> in its home harbor, which then lost crucial naval battles; so that in the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_chronology" title="Minoan chronology">LMIB/LMII</a> event (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1450 BC) the cities of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> burned and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a> took over <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>. If the eruption occurred in the late 17th century BC (as most chronologists now think) then its immediate effects belong to the Middle to Late Bronze Age transition, and not to the end of the Late Bronze Age, but it could have triggered the instability that led to the collapse first of Knossos and then of Bronze Age society overall.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One such theory highlights the role of Cretan expertise in administering the empire, post—Thera. If this expertise was concentrated in Crete, then the Mycenaeans may have made political and commercial mistakes in administering the Cretan empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Archaeological findings, including some on the island of Thera, suggest that the center of the Minoan civilization at the time of the eruption was actually on Thera rather than on Crete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> According to this theory, the catastrophic loss of the political, administrative and economic center due to the eruption, as well as the damage wrought by the tsunami to the coastal towns and villages of Crete precipitated the decline of the Minoans. A weakened political entity with a reduced economic and military capability and fabled riches would have then been more vulnerable to conquest. Indeed, the Santorini eruption is usually dated to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1630 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> while the Mycenaean Greeks first enter the historical record a few decades later, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The later Mycenaean assaults on Crete (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1450 BC) and Troy (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1250 BC) would have been a continuation of the steady encroachment of the Greeks upon the weakened Minoan world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkans">Balkans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Balkans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:208px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valchitran-treasure.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="844" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Valchitran-treasure.jpg/206px-Valchitran-treasure.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Valchitran-treasure.jpg/309px-Valchitran-treasure.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Valchitran-treasure.jpg/412px-Valchitran-treasure.jpg 2x" width="206"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valchitran-treasure.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Valchitran_Treasure" title="Valchitran Treasure">Valchitran Treasure</a> from 1600 to 1100 BC</div></div></div>
<p>A study in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Antiquity_(journal)" title="Antiquity (journal)">Antiquity</a></i> published in 2013 reported the discovery of a tin bronze foil from the <a href="/wiki/Plo%C4%8Dnik_(archaeological_site)" title="Pločnik (archaeological site)">Pločnik archaeological site</a> securely dated to c. 4650 BC as well as 14 other artifacts from <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> dated to before 4000 BC has shown that early tin bronze was more common than previously thought, and developed independently in Europe 1500 years before the first tin bronze alloys in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>. The production of complex tin bronzes lasted for c. 500 years in the Balkans. The authors reported that evidence for the production of such complex bronzes disappears at the end of the 5th millennium coinciding with the "collapse of large cultural complexes in north-eastern Bulgaria and Thrace in the late fifth millennium BC". Tin bronzes using <a href="/wiki/Cassiterite" title="Cassiterite">cassiterite</a> tin would be reintroduced to the area again some 1500 years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jovanovic_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-jovanovic-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dabene_Treasure" title="Dabene Treasure">Dabene Treasure</a> was unearthed from 2004 to 2007 near <a href="/wiki/Karlovo" title="Karlovo">Karlovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plovdiv_Province" title="Plovdiv Province">Plovdiv Province</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>. The whole treasure consists of 20,000 gold jewelerry items from 18 to 23 carats. The most important of them was a dagger made of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> with an unusual edge. The treasure was dated to the end of the 3rd millennium B.C. The scientists suggest that the Karlovo valley used to be a major crafts center which exported golden jewelry all over Europe. It is considered as one of the largest prehistoric golden treasure in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Europe">Central Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Central Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebra_disc_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3385" data-file-width="4177" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nebra_disc_1.jpg/205px-Nebra_disc_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nebra_disc_1.jpg/308px-Nebra_disc_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nebra_disc_1.jpg/410px-Nebra_disc_1.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebra_disc_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nebra_sky_disc" title="Nebra sky disc">Nebra sky disc</a>, Germany, 1800–1600 BC</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Southeastern_Europe#Bronze_Age" title="Prehistory of Southeastern Europe">Prehistory of Southeastern Europe § Bronze Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_in_Romania" title="Bronze Age in Romania">Bronze Age in Romania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1352" data-file-width="1930" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG/205px-Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG/308px-Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG/410px-Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG 2x" width="205"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuirasses_Marmesse.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Urnfield_culture" title="Urnfield culture">Urnfield culture cuirasses</a>, France, 9th century BC.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, the early Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Unetice_culture" title="Unetice culture">Unetice culture</a> (2300–1600 BC) includes numerous smaller groups like the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Straubingen_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Straubingen culture (page does not exist)">Straubing</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Adlerberg_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Adlerberg culture (page does not exist)">Adlerberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hatvan" title="Hatvan">Hatvan</a> cultures. Some very rich burials, such as the one located at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leubingen" title="Leubingen">Leubingen</a> with grave gifts crafted from gold, point to an increase of social stratification already present in the Unetice culture. All in all, cemeteries of this period are rare and of small size. The Unetice culture is followed by the middle Bronze Age (1600–1200 BC) <a href="/wiki/Tumulus_culture" title="Tumulus culture">Tumulus culture</a>, which is characterised by inhumation burials in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumuli" title="Tumuli">tumuli</a> (barrows). In the eastern <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C3%B6r%C3%B6s_River" title="Körös River">Körös</a> tributaries, the early Bronze Age first saw the introduction of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mako_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mako culture (page does not exist)">Mako culture</a>, followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otomani_culture" title="Otomani culture">Otomani</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gyulavarsand_culture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gyulavarsand culture (page does not exist)">Gyulavarsand cultures</a>.
</p><p>The late Bronze Age <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urnfield" title="Urnfield">Urnfield</a> culture (1300–700 BC) is characterized by cremation burials. It includes the <a href="/wiki/Lusatian_culture" title="Lusatian culture">Lusatian culture</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (1300–500 BC) that continues into the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. The Central European Bronze Age is followed by the Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> (700–450 BC).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_archaeological_sites" title="List of archaeological sites">Important sites</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Biskupin" title="Biskupin">Biskupin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nebra_(Unstrut)" title="Nebra (Unstrut)">Nebra</a> (<a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vr%C3%A1ble" title="Vráble">Vráble</a> (<a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>)</li>
<li>Zug-Sumpf, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Zug" title="Canton of Zug">Zug</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></li></ul>
<p>The Bronze Age in Central Europe has been described in the chronological schema of German prehistorian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Reinecke" title="Paul Reinecke">Paul Reinecke</a>. He described Bronze A1 (Bz A1) period (2300–2000 BC: triangular daggers, flat axes, stone wrist-guards, flint arrowheads) and Bronze A2 (Bz A2) period (1950–1700 BC: daggers with metal hilt, flanged axes, halberds, pins with perforated spherical heads, solid bracelets) and phases Hallstatt A and B (Ha A and B).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Europe">South Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: South Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:104px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="1520" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg/102px-Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg/153px-Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg/205px-Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg 2x" width="102"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronzetto_nuragico_Sulcis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nuragic_civilization" title="Nuragic civilization">Nuragic</a> figurine, Sardinia, c. 1000 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apennine_culture" title="Apennine culture">Apennine culture</a> (also called Italian Bronze Age) is a technology complex of central and southern Italy spanning the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> and Bronze Age proper. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camuni" title="Camuni">Camuni</a> were an ancient people of uncertain origin (according to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, they were <a href="/wiki/Euganei" title="Euganei">Euganei</a>; according to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, they were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhaetians" title="Rhaetians">Rhaetians</a>) who lived in <a href="/wiki/Val_Camonica" title="Val Camonica">Val Camonica</a>—in what is now northern <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a>—during the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, although human groups of hunters, shepherds and farmers are known to have lived in the area since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>.
</p><p>Located in <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nuragic_civilization" title="Nuragic civilization">Nuragic civilization</a> lasted from the early Bronze Age (18th century BC) to the 2nd century AD, when the islands were already Romanized. They take their name from the characteristic Nuragic towers, which evolved from the pre-existing megalithic culture, which built <a href="/wiki/Dolmen" title="Dolmen">dolmens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menhir" title="Menhir">menhirs</a>. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg/205px-Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg/308px-Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg/410px-Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tesoro_de_Villena.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Treasure_of_Villena" title="Treasure of Villena">Treasure of Vilena</a>, Spain, 1000 BC.</div></div></div>
<p>The nuraghe towers are unanimously considered the best-preserved and largest megalithic remains in Europe. Their effective use is still debated: some scholars considered them as monumental tombs, others as <a href="/wiki/Giants%27_grave" title="Giants' grave">Houses of the Giants</a>, other as fortresses, ovens for metal fusion, prisons or, finally, temples for a solar cult. Around the end of the 3rd millennium BC, Sardinia exported towards Sicily a <i>Culture</i> that built small dolmens, trilithic or polygonal shaped, that served as tombs as it has been ascertained in the Sicilian dolmen of "Cava dei Servi". From this region, they reached Malta island and other countries of Mediterranean basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terramare" title="Terramare">Terramare</a> was an early <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> civilization in the area of what is now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pianura_Padana" title="Pianura Padana">Pianura Padana</a> (northern Italy) before the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> and in other parts of Europe. They lived in square villages of wooden <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stilt_houses" title="Stilt houses">stilt houses</a>. These villages were built on land, but generally near a stream, with roads that <a href="/wiki/Grid_plan" title="Grid plan">crossed each other at right angles</a>. The whole complex denoted the nature of a fortified settlement. Terramare was widespread in the Pianura Padana (especially along the <a href="/wiki/Panaro_(river)" title="Panaro (river)">Panaro</a> river, between <a href="/wiki/Modena" title="Modena">Modena</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>) and in the rest of Europe. The civilization developed in the Middle and Late Bronze Age, between the 17th and the 13th centuries BC.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Castellieri_culture" title="Castellieri culture">Castellieri culture</a> developed in <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">Istria</a> during the Middle Bronze Age. It lasted for more than a millennium, from the 15th century BC until the Roman conquest in the 3rd century BC. It takes its name from the fortified boroughs (<i>Castellieri</i>, <a href="/wiki/Friulian_language" title="Friulian language">Friulian</a>: <i>cjastelir</i>) that characterized the culture. The <a href="/wiki/Canegrate_culture" title="Canegrate culture">Canegrate culture</a> developed from the mid-Bronze Age (13th century BC) until the Iron Age in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pianura_Padana" title="Pianura Padana">Pianura Padana</a>, in what are now western <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Piedmont" title="Piedmont">Piedmont</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_Ticino" title="Canton Ticino">Ticino</a>. It takes its name from the township of <a href="/wiki/Canegrate" title="Canegrate">Canegrate</a> where, in the 20th century, some fifty tombs with ceramics and metal objects were found. The Canegrate culture migrated from the northwest part of the Alps and descended to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pianura_Padana" title="Pianura Padana">Pianura Padana</a> from the Swiss Alps passes and the Ticino.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Golasecca_culture" title="Golasecca culture">Golasecca culture</a> developed starting from the late Bronze Age in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Po_plain" title="Po plain">Po plain</a>. It takes its name from Golasecca, a locality next to the <a href="/wiki/Ticino" title="Ticino">Ticino</a> where, in the early 19th century, abbot Giovanni Battista Giani excavated its first findings (some fifty tombs with ceramics and metal objects). Remains of the Golasecca culture span an area of c. 20,000 square kilometers south to the Alps, between the Po, <a href="/wiki/Sesia" title="Sesia">Sesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serio_(river)" title="Serio (river)">Serio</a> rivers, dating from the 9th to the 4th century BC.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Europe">West Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: West Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Britain">Great Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Great Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_Britain" title="Bronze Age Britain">Bronze Age Britain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:179px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_sheild,_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1159" data-file-width="1170" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg/177px-Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg/266px-Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg/354px-Bronze_sheild%2C_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg 2x" width="177"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_sheild,_1200-700_BC_British_Museum_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze shield, 1200–700 BC</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, the Bronze Age is considered to have been the period from around 2100 to 750 BC. <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">Migration</a> brought new people to the islands from the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamnaya_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yamnaya-86">[86]</a></sup> Recent tooth enamel isotope research on bodies found in early Bronze Age graves around <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> indicates that at least some of the migrants came from the area of modern <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>. Another example site is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Must_Farm" title="Must Farm">Must Farm</a>, near Whittlesey, which has recently been host to the most complete Bronze Age wheel ever to be found. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beaker_culture" title="Beaker culture">Beaker culture</a> displayed different behaviors from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people, and cultural change was significant. Integration is thought to have been peaceful, as many of the early <a href="/wiki/Henge" title="Henge">henge</a> sites were seemingly adopted by the newcomers. The rich <a href="/wiki/Wessex_culture" title="Wessex culture">Wessex culture</a> developed in southern Britain at this time. Additionally, the climate was deteriorating; where once the weather was warm and dry it became much wetter as the Bronze Age continued, forcing the population away from easily defended sites in the hills and into the fertile <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a>. Large livestock farms developed in the lowlands and appear to have contributed to economic growth and inspired increasing forest clearances. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deverel-Rimbury_culture" title="Deverel-Rimbury culture">Deverel-Rimbury culture</a> began to emerge in the second half of the Middle Bronze Age (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1400–1100 BC) to exploit these conditions. <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> were major sources of <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> for much of western Europe and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> was extracted from sites such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Orme" title="Great Orme">Great Orme</a> mine in northern <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. Social groups appear to have been tribal but with growing complexity and hierarchies becoming apparent.
</p><p>The burial of the dead (which, until this period, had usually been communal) became more individual. For example, whereas in the Neolithic a large <a href="/wiki/Chambered_cairn" title="Chambered cairn">chambered cairn</a> or <a href="/wiki/Long_barrow" title="Long barrow">long barrow</a> housed the dead, Early Bronze Age people buried their dead in individual <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">barrows</a> (also commonly known and marked on modern British <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Survey" title="Ordnance Survey">Ordnance Survey</a> maps as tumuli), or sometimes in <a href="/wiki/Cist" title="Cist">cists</a> covered with <a href="/wiki/Cairn" title="Cairn">cairns</a>.
</p><p>The greatest quantities of bronze objects in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> were discovered in <a href="/wiki/East_Cambridgeshire" title="East Cambridgeshire">East Cambridgeshire</a>, where the most important finds were recovered in <a href="/wiki/Isleham" title="Isleham">Isleham</a> (more than <a href="/wiki/Isleham_Hoard" title="Isleham Hoard">6500 pieces</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
Alloying of copper with zinc or tin to make <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> was practiced soon after the discovery of copper itself. One copper mine at <a href="/wiki/Great_Orme" title="Great Orme">Great Orme</a> in North Wales, extended to a depth of 70 meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Alderley_Edge" title="Alderley Edge">Alderley Edge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, carbon dates have established mining at around 2280 to 1890 BC (at 95% probability).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The earliest identified <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a> site (Sigwells, Somerset) is much later, dated by Globular Urn style pottery to approximately the 12th century BC. The identifiable sherds from over 500 mould fragments included a perfect fit of the hilt of a sword in the Wilburton style held in Somerset County Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabor_2008_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabor_2008-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_Bronze_Age">Atlantic Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Atlantic Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:158px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_(24466600427).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg/156px-Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg/234px-Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg/312px-Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_%2824466600427%29.jpg 2x" width="156"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zwaard_van_Jutphaas_(24466600427).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sword_of_Jutphaas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sword of Jutphaas (page does not exist)">Sword of Jutphaas</a><sup class="noprint" style="font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zwaard_van_Jutphaas" title="nl:Zwaard van Jutphaas">nl</a>]</sup>, <a href="/wiki/Hilversum_culture" title="Hilversum culture">Netherlands</a>, c. 1500 BC</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Bronze_Age" title="Atlantic Bronze Age">Atlantic Bronze Age</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Bronze_Age" title="Atlantic Bronze Age">Atlantic Bronze Age</a> is a cultural complex of the period of approximately 1300–700 BC that includes different cultures in Portugal, Andalusia, Galicia, and the British Isles. It is marked by economic and cultural exchange. Commercial contacts extend to Denmark and the Mediterranean. The Atlantic Bronze Age was defined by many distinct regional centers of metal production, unified by a regular maritime exchange of some of their products.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Ireland#Bronze_Age" title="Prehistoric Ireland">Prehistoric Ireland § Bronze Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Bronze_Age" title="Atlantic Bronze Age">Atlantic Bronze Age</a></div>
<p>The Bronze Age in Ireland commenced around 2000 BC when copper was alloyed with tin and used to manufacture Ballybeg type flat axes and associated metalwork. The preceding period is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_Age" title="Copper Age">Copper Age</a> and is characterised by the production of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Flat_axes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Flat axes (page does not exist)">flat axes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daggers" title="Daggers">daggers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halberds" title="Halberds">halberds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stitching_awl" title="Stitching awl">awls</a> in copper. The period is divided into three phases: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a> (2000–1500 BC), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a> (1500–1200 BC), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a> (1200–<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 500 BC). Ireland is also known for a relatively large number of Early Bronze Age burials.
</p><p>One of the characteristic types of artifact of the Early Bronze Age in Ireland is the flat axe. There are five main types of flat axes: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lough_Ravel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lough Ravel (page does not exist)">Lough Ravel</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2200 BC), Ballybeg (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2000 BC), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Killaha&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Killaha (page does not exist)">Killaha</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2000 BC), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ballyvalley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ballyvalley (page does not exist)">Ballyvalley</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2000–1600 BC), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Derryniggin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Derryniggin (page does not exist)">Derryniggin</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1600 BC), and a number of metal ingots in the shape of axes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Europe">North Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: North Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:205px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solvognen-00100.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4550" data-file-width="6088" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Solvognen-00100.jpg/203px-Solvognen-00100.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Solvognen-00100.jpg/305px-Solvognen-00100.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Solvognen-00100.jpg/406px-Solvognen-00100.jpg 2x" width="203"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solvognen-00100.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trundholm_sun_chariot" title="Trundholm sun chariot">Trundholm sun chariot</a>, Denmark, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1500 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The Bronze Age in Northern Europe spans the entire <a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BC" title="2nd millennium BC">2nd millennium BC</a> (<a href="/wiki/Unetice_culture" title="Unetice culture">Unetice culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urnfield_culture" title="Urnfield culture">Urnfield culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tumulus_culture" title="Tumulus culture">Tumulus culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terramare_culture" title="Terramare culture">Terramare culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lusatian_culture" title="Lusatian culture">Lusatian culture</a>) lasting until <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 600 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Northern Bronze Age</a> was both a period and a Bronze Age culture in Scandinavian pre-history, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1700–500 BC, with sites that reached as far east as Estonia. Succeeding the Late Neolithic culture, its ethnic and linguistic affinities are unknown in the absence of written sources. It is followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_Iron_Age" title="Pre-Roman Iron Age">Pre-Roman Iron Age</a>.
</p><p>Even though Northern European Bronze Age cultures were relatively late, and came into existence via trade, sites present rich and well-preserved objects made of wool, wood and imported Central European bronze and gold. Many rock carvings depict ships, and the large stone burial monuments known as stone ships suggest that shipping played an important role. Thousands of rock carvings depict ships, most probably representing sewn plank built canoes for warfare, fishing, and trade. These may have a history as far back as the neolithic period and continue into the Pre-Roman Iron Age, as shown by the <a href="/wiki/Hjortspring_boat" title="Hjortspring boat">Hjortspring boat</a>. There are many mounds and rock carving sites from the period. Numerous artifacts of bronze and gold are found. No written language existed in the Nordic countries during the Bronze Age. The rock carvings have been dated through comparison with depicted artifacts.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caucasus">Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arsenical_bronze" title="Arsenical bronze">Arsenical bronze</a> artifacts of the <a href="/wiki/Maykop_culture" title="Maykop culture">Maykop culture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> have been dated around the 4th millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> This innovation resulted in the circulation of arsenical bronze technology over southern and eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Pontic.E2.80.93Caspian_steppe"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pontic–Caspian_steppe">Pontic–Caspian steppe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Pontic–Caspian steppe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1372" data-file-width="3326" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/320px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/480px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg/640px-Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yamnaya_Steppe_Pastoralists.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze Age spread of <a href="/wiki/Yamnaya_culture" title="Yamnaya culture">Yamnaya</a> <a href="/wiki/Western_Steppe_Herders" title="Western Steppe Herders">steppe pastoralist</a> ancestry into two subcontinents—Europe and South Asia, and location of the <a href="/wiki/Afanasievo_culture" title="Afanasievo culture">Afanasievo culture</a>, which has the same genetic characteristics as the Yamnayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamnaya_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yamnaya-86">[86]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yamnaya_culture" title="Yamnaya culture">Yamnaya culture</a> is a Late Copper Age/Early Bronze Age culture of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Bug" title="Southern Bug">Southern Bug</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dniester" title="Dniester">Dniester</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural</a> region (<a href="/wiki/Pontic%E2%80%93Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic–Caspian steppe">the Pontic steppe</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> dating to the 36th–23rd centuries BC. The name also appears in English as Pit-Grave Culture or Ochre-Grave Culture. The <a href="/wiki/Catacomb_culture" title="Catacomb culture">Catacomb culture</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2800–2200 BC, comprises several related Early Bronze Age cultures occupying what is presently <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Srubnaya_culture" title="Srubnaya culture">Srubnaya culture</a> was a Late Bronze Age (18th–12th centuries BC) culture. It is a successor to the Yamnaya and the <a href="/wiki/Poltavka_culture" title="Poltavka culture">Poltavka culture</a>.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks" style="width:20.0em;border-spacing:0;padding:0;"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:World_history" title="Category:World history">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-title-with-pretitle" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;line-height:1.0em;"><a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">Human history</a><br/><span style="font-size:80%;">and <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a></span></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-above" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;background:#efefef;text-align:left;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Copper_metallurgy_in_Africa" title="Copper metallurgy in Africa">Copper metallurgy in Africa</a></div>
<p>Iron and copper smelting appeared around the same time in most parts of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springer_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Springer-96">[96]</a></sup> As such, most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_African_Civilization" title="Classical African Civilization">African civilizations</a> outside of Egypt did not experience a distinct Bronze Age. Evidence for iron smelting appears earlier or at the same time as copper smelting in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> c. 900–800 BC, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> c. 700–500 BC and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> c. 300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springer_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Springer-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a longstanding debate about whether the development of both copper and iron metallurgy were independently developed in sub-Saharan Africa or were introduced from the outside across the Sahara Desert from North Africa or the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springer_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Springer-96">[96]</a></sup> Evidence for theories of independent development and outside introduction are scarce and subject to active scholarly debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Springer_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-Springer-96">[96]</a></sup> Scholars have suggested that both the relative dearth of archeological research in sub-Saharan Africa as well as long-standing prejudices have limited or biased our understanding of pre-historic metallurgy on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-100">[100]</a></sup> One scholar characterized the state of historical knowledge as such: "To say that the history of metallurgy in sub-Saharan Africa is complicated is perhaps an understatement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Africa">West Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: West Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Copper smelting took place in West Africa prior to the appearance of iron smelting in the region. Evidence for copper smelting furnaces was found near <a href="/wiki/Agadez" title="Agadez">Agadez</a>, Niger that has been dated as early as 2200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup> However, evidence for copper production in this region before 1000 BC is debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup> Evidence of copper mining and smelting has been found at <a href="/wiki/Akjoujt" title="Akjoujt">Akjoujt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a> that suggests small scale production <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 800 to 400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-4"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy_in_pre-Columbian_America" title="Metallurgy in pre-Columbian America">Metallurgy in pre-Columbian America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy_in_pre-Columbian_Mesoamerica" title="Metallurgy in pre-Columbian Mesoamerica">Metallurgy in pre-Columbian Mesoamerica</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moche_culture" title="Moche culture">Moche</a> civilization of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> independently discovered and developed bronze smelting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Bronze technology was developed further by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Incas</a> and used widely both for utilitarian objects and sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> A later appearance of limited bronze smelting in West <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> suggests either contact of that region with <a href="/wiki/Andean_civilizations" title="Andean civilizations">Andean cultures</a> or separate discovery of the technology. The <a href="/wiki/Calchaqu%C3%AD" title="Calchaquí">Calchaquí</a> people of Northwest <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> had bronze technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bronze_Age&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Trade and industry played a major role in the development of the ancient Bronze Age civilizations. With artifacts of the Indus Valley civilization being found in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, it is clear that these civilizations were not only in touch with each other but also trading with each other. Early long-distance trade was limited almost exclusively to luxury goods like spices, textiles and precious metals. Not only did this make cities with ample amounts of these products extremely rich but also led to an intermingling of cultures for the first time in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kristiansen_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-kristiansen-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade routes were not only over land but also over water. The first and most extensive trade routes were over rivers such as the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> which led to growth of cities on the banks of these rivers. The domestication of camels at a later time also helped encourage the use of trade routes over land, linking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>. This further led to towns sprouting up in numbers anywhere and everywhere there was a pit-stop or caravan-to-ship port.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bronze was independently discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Maykop_culture" title="Maykop culture">Maykop culture</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> as early as the mid-<a href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BC" title="4th millennium BC">4th millennium BC</a>, which makes them the producers of the oldest-known bronze. However, the Maykop culture only had <a href="/wiki/Arsenical_bronze" title="Arsenical bronze">arsenical bronze</a>. Other regions developed bronze and its associated technology at different periods.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-jovanovic-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-jovanovic_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-jovanovic_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRadivojevicRehrenKuzmanovic-CvetkovicJovanovic2013">Radivojevic, M; Rehren, T; Kuzmanovic-Cvetkovic, J; Jovanovic, M; Northover, JP (2013). <a class="external text" href="http://discovery.ucl.ac.uk/1420706/" rel="nofollow">"Tainted ores and the rise of tin bronzes in Eurasia, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>6500 years ago"</a>. <i>Antiquity</i>. <b>87</b> (338): 1030–1045. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2FS0003598X0004984X" rel="nofollow">10.1017/S0003598X0004984X</a></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Antiquity&amp;rft.atitle=Tainted+ores+and+the+rise+of+tin+bronzes+in+Eurasia%2C+%3Cabbr+title%3D%22circa%22%3Ec.%3C%2Fabbr%3E6500+years+ago&amp;rft.volume=87&amp;rft.issue=338&amp;rft.pages=1030-1045&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FS0003598X0004984X&amp;rft.aulast=Radivojevic&amp;rft.aufirst=M&amp;rft.au=Rehren%2C+T&amp;rft.au=Kuzmanovic-Cvetkovic%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Jovanovic%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Northover%2C+JP&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdiscovery.ucl.ac.uk%2F1420706%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABronze+Age"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-AK2013-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AK2013_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFKillebrew2013">Killebrew, Ann E. (2013), <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150903212143/https://books.google.com/books?id=gBCl2IQfNioC&amp;pg=PA1" rel="nofollow">"The Philistines and Other "Sea Peoples" in Text and Archaeology"</a>, <i>Society of Biblical Literature Archaeology and biblical studies</i>, Society of Biblical Lit, vol. 15, p. 2, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-58983-721-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-58983-721-8"><bdi>978-1-58983-721-8</bdi></a>, archived from <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=gBCl2IQfNioC&amp;pg=PA1" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 3 September 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 June</span> 2015</span></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Society+of+Biblical+Literature+Archaeology+and+biblical+studies&amp;rft.atitle=The+Philistines+and+Other+%22Sea+Peoples%22+in+Text+and+Archaeology&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-58983-721-8&amp;rft.aulast=Killebrew&amp;rft.aufirst=Ann+E.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DgBCl2IQfNioC%26pg%3DPA1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABronze+Age"></span>. Quote: "First coined in 1881 by the French Egyptologist G. Maspero (1896), the somewhat misleading term "Sea Peoples" encompasses the ethnonyms Lukka, Sherden, Shekelesh, Teresh, Eqwesh, Denyen, Sikil / Tjekker, Weshesh, and Peleset (Philistines). [Footnote: The modern term "Sea Peoples" refers to peoples that appear in several New Kingdom Egyptian texts as originating from "islands" (tables 1–2; Adams and Cohen, this volume; see, e.g., <a href="/wiki/Robert_Drews" title="Robert Drews">Drews</a> 1993, 57 for a summary). The use of quotation marks in association with the term "Sea Peoples" in our title is intended to draw attention to the problematic nature of this commonly used term. It is noteworthy that the designation "of the sea" appears only concerning the Sherden, Shekelesh, and Eqwesh. Subsequently, this term was applied somewhat indiscriminately to several additional ethnonyms, including the Philistines, who are portrayed in their earliest appearance as invaders from the north during the reigns of Merenptah and Ramesses Ill (see, e.g., Sandars 1978; Redford 1992, 243, n. 14; for a recent review of the primary and secondary literature, see Woudhuizen 2006). Henceforth the term Sea Peoples will appear without quotation marks.]"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Drews48-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Drews48_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bFpK6aXEWN8C&amp;pg=PA48" rel="nofollow"><i>The End of the Bronze Age: Changes in Warfare and the Catastrophe Ca. 1200 B.C.</i>, Robert Drews, pp. 48–61</a> Quote: "The thesis that a great "migration of the Sea Peoples" occurred ca. 1200 B.C. is supposedly based on Egyptian inscriptions, one from the reign of Merneptah and another from the reign of Ramesses III. Yet in the inscriptions themselves, such a migration nowhere appears. After reviewing what the Egyptian texts have to say about 'the sea peoples', one Egyptologist (Wolfgang Helck) recently remarked that although some things are unclear, "eins ist aber sicher: Nach den agyptischen Texten Haben wir es Nicht mit einer 'Volkerwanderung' zu tun." Thus the migration hypothesis is based not on the inscriptions themselves but their interpretation."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-KSnPG-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-KSnPG_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-KSnPG_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Karin Sowada and Peter Grave. </li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The archaeological term "Bronze Age" was first introduced for Europe in the 1830s and soon extended to the Near East. By the 1860s, there was some debate as to whether the term should be extended to China (<a href="/wiki/John_Lubbock,_1st_Baron_Avebury" title="John Lubbock, 1st Baron Avebury">John Lubbock</a>, <i>Prehistoric Times</i> (1868), cited after <i>The Athenaeum</i> No. 2121, 20 June 1868, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.ch/books?id=VpRGAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA870" rel="nofollow">p. 870</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Robert L. Thorp, <i>China in the Early Bronze Age: Shang Civilization</i>, University of Pennsylvania Press (2013).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">" Without entering on the vexed question whether or not there ever was a bronze age in any part of the world distinguished by the sole use of that metal, it is a fact that in China and Japan to the present day, amid an iron age, bronze is in constant use for cutting instruments, either alone or in combination with steel."
<i>The Rectangular Review</i>, Volume 1 (1871), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.ch/books?id=CXwtAQAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA408" rel="nofollow">p. 408</a>.</span>
